Two Origins of Man in the Quran: Remote and Proximate
Classification of human origin into remote and proximate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the remote origin (Adam) from the proximate origin (womb development) in the Quranic account of creation.

Core Idea
The Quran teaches that humanity's remote origin is one soul (Adam), while each person's proximate origin is in the womb.

In studying the Quranic narrative of human creation, a key epistemological tool is to distinguish between two levels of origin: the remote origin (al-aṣl al-baʿīd) and the proximate origin (al-aṣl al-qarīb). This classification helps us understand how the Quran speaks about the beginning of the human species and the formation of each individual. In this lesson, you will learn to apply this distinction to Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'origin' (aṣl) in the passage
The author uses aṣl to refer to the source or beginning. Locate the two instances: 'remote origin' and 'proximate origin'.
The term appears in the first sentence: 'يرجع إلى أصلين'.
2
Distinguish the two categories: remote vs. proximate
Remote means the first beginning of the species (Adam), while proximate means the biological development within the mother's womb.
These are two distinct levels, not contradictory.
3
Locate the Quranic evidence for each origin
The remote origin is the single soul (Adam), from whom Eve is derived. The proximate origin is alluded to by 'formation in the womb'.
The author does not cite specific verses here, but later verses may be referenced.
4
Apply this distinction to other Quranic verses
For example, Quran 4:1 (creation from a single soul) refers to the remote origin; Quran 76:2 (creation from a drop) refers to the proximate origin.
Practice identifying which origin each verse addresses.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمكن تصنيف طبيعة خلق الإنسان بإرجاعه إلى أصلين: أحدهما بعيد ويعني بداية الخليقة الإنسانية، والآخر قريب ويعني طبيعة تخلقه في رحم أمه. وقد أشار القرآن الكريم إلى أن عالم الأنفس بالنسبة إلى الأصل البعيد مكوّن من نفس واحدة تتمثل في آدم عليه السلام ثم اشتقت منه زوجه حواء، فتأسست الأسرة الإنسانية بعد ذلك:
The nature of the creation of the human being can be classified by referring it to two origins: one remote, meaning the beginning of the human creation, and the other proximate, meaning the nature of his formation in his mother's womb. The Noble Quran has indicated that the world of souls with respect to the remote origin is composed of a single soul represented by Adam, peace be upon him, then his wife Eve was derived from him, and then the human family was established afterward:
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a classificatory passage that divides the origin of the human being into two categories: a remote origin and a proximate origin. The remote origin refers to the very beginning of human creation, which the Quran identifies as the single soul of Adam (Adam). From him, his wife Eve (Hawwa') was derived, establishing the human family. The proximate origin refers to the biological development within the mother's womb. This classification is epistemological in that it organizes the Quranic data about creation into a conceptual framework. The author is setting the stage for a more detailed discussion of the verses related to the origin of man.

Key Terms
أصل بعيد (aṣl ba'īd): Remote origin; the first beginning of the human species, i.e., the creation of Adam.
أصل قريب (aṣl qarīb): Proximate origin; the biological formation and development of the individual human in the womb.
عالم الأنفس ('ālam al-anfus): The world of souls; here refers to the realm of human souls or the human race.
نفس واحدة (nafs wāḥida): A single soul; the first human soul (Adam) from which the entire human race originates.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic account of human creation is organized around two distinct origins: the remote origin of the species in Adam, and the proximate origin of each person in the womb.

The Two Origins of Man in the Quran
Author classifies human origin into remote and near with Quranic evidence and exegetical discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the remote origin (aṣl baʿīd) and near origin (aṣl qarīb) of man from Quranic evidence and explain how this classification informs Islamic educational principles.

Core Idea
The Quran classifies human origin into remote (Adam) and near (embryo), revealing the integration of spiritual and physical dimensions.

In this passage, the author presents a Quran-based classification of human origin that distinguishes between the beginning of humanity (Adam) and the individual's formation in the womb. This classification is not merely descriptive; it carries implications for how Islamic education should address human needs and counter materialist ideologies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two origins
Read the author's statement: 'The origin of man can be classified by referring it to two origins: one remote and one near.' Note the definitions: remote means the beginning of human creation, near means embryonic development.
The remote origin is also linked to the concept of 'one soul' (nafs wāḥida) in Quran 4:1.
2
Find Quranic evidence for each origin
Locate the two Quranic verses cited: Surah An-Nisa 4:1 for the remote origin (creation from a single soul) and Surah Al-An'am 6:98 for the near origin (mention of mustaqarr and mustawdaʿ). Understand how each verse relates to the respective origin.
The author pairs them to show the continuity of creation.
3
Analyze the exegetical dispute on mustaqarr and mustawdaʿ
Notice that commentators differed on the meanings of mustaqarr and mustawdaʿ. The author concludes that mustaqarr refers to the womb (female) and mustawdaʿ to the loins (male), following Ibn Jarir, Ibn 'Ashur, and Qutb. Recognize that the dispute does not negate the dual-origin framework.
This step trains you to handle scholarly disagreement without losing the core classification.
4
Derive implications for Islamic education
Read the author's conclusion: Islamic education must care for man as a human creation, provide knowledge consistent with his origin, and reject evolutionist claims. Connect the dual origin to the integration of opposing lines in human nature.
The remote origin gives human dignity; the near origin gives individual development.

أصل الإنسان يمكن تصنيف طبيعة خلق الإنسان بإرجاعه إلى أصلين: أحدهما بعيد ويعني بداية الخليقة الإنسانية، والآخر قريب ويعني طبيعة تخلقه في رحم أمه. وقد أشار القرآن الكريم إلى أن عالم الأنفس بالنسبة إلى الأصل البعيد مكوّن من نفس واحدة تتمثل في آدم عليه السلام ثم اشتقت منه زوجـه حـواء)، فتأسست الأسرة الإنسانية بعد ذلك: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا النَّاسُ اتَّقُوا رَبَّكُمُ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ مِنْ نَفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَخَلَقَ مِنْهَا زَوْجَهَا وَبَثَّ مِنْهُمَا رِجَالاً كَثِيرًا وَنِسَاءً وَاتَّقُوا اللهَ الَّذِي تَسَاءَلُونَ بِهِ وَالأَرْحَامَ إِنَّ اللَّهَ كَانَ عَلَيْكُمْ رَقِيبًا [النساء: ١/٤]. (۱) المرجع السابق، ١٤٠ - ١٤٢. (۲) الطبري، تفسير الطبري، ٥٦٥/٣ - ٥٦٦ . وتجدر الإشارة هنا إلى أن سيد قطب لا يرى أن هناك أي أثر إسلامي معتمد في قصة خلق حواء من آدم بما يأتي تفسيراً لقوله تعالى: (من نفس واحدة ويرجح أنها نفس واحدة لاتحاد الذكر والأنثى في الكنه والحقيقة (الظلال ١١٥٩/٢، الهامش ١). وبالنسبة إلى الأصل القريب فإن القرآن يعمد في إشارته إلى ذلك إلى إقرانه بالأصل البعيد: ﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي أَنْشَأَكُمْ مِنْ نَفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ فَمُسْتَقَرٌّ وَمُسْتَوْدَعْ قَدْ فَصَّلْنَا الآياتِ لِقَوْمٍ يَفْقَهُونَ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٩٨/٦]. وعلى الرغم من الخلاف الدائر بين المفسرين على معنى (المستقر والمستودع إلا أن اتساع الخلاف لا يخرج عن كون المستقر في الرحم إنما هو بالنسبة إلى الأنثى، والمستودع في الصلب إنما هو بالنسبة للرجل. وكلا المعنيين مشمول بهذه الآية، كما ذهب إلى ذلك ابن جرير (۱) ، وتابعه ابن عاشور (۳) ، ورجح هـذ الاختيار سيد قطب (۳). وإذا عرفنا تقرير القرآن لأصل الإنسان على هذا النحو، فإن التربية الإسلامية معنية بالاهتمام بالإنسان بوصفه مخلوقاً آدمياً يستحق أن تقدم لـه المعارف الملبية لحاجاته الآدمية الإنسانية المتسقة مع أصل خلقته، بعيداً عن الشطط الذي أوقع أصحابه في القول بتطور الإنسان ونشأته من أصل جرثومي، حتى صار قرداً، ثم إنساناً. ومن جانب آخر فإن في ذلك إشارة إلى الطبيعة الإنسانية من حيث تكامل الخطوط المتقابلة فيها، المتمثلة في الأصل البعيد، والأصل القريب.
The origin of man. The nature of the creation of man can be classified by referring it to two origins: one remote, meaning the beginning of human creation, and the other near, meaning the nature of his formation in his mother's womb. And the Noble Qur'an has indicated that the world of souls with respect to the remote origin is composed of one soul represented by Adam, peace be upon him, then his wife Eve was derived from him, and the human family was founded thereafter: [Quran 4:1]. (1) Previous reference, 140-142. (2) Al-Tabari, Tafsir al-Tabari, 3/565-566. It is worth noting here that Sayyid Qutb does not see that there is any reliable Islamic trace in the story of the creation of Eve from Adam as an interpretation of His statement: 'from one soul' and he prefers that it is one soul due to the unity of male and female in essence and reality (al-Zilal 2/1159, footnote 1). And with respect to the near origin, the Qur'an in its indication to that pairs it with the remote origin: [Quran 6:98]. And despite the dispute among the commentators on the meaning of 'mustaqarr' and 'mustawdaʿ', the breadth of the dispute does not go beyond that 'mustaqarr' is in the womb with respect to the female, and 'mustawdaʿ' is in the loins with respect to the male. And both meanings are included in this verse, as held by Ibn Jarir, followed by Ibn 'Ashur, and this choice was preferred by Sayyid Qutb. And if we know the Quran's statement on the origin of man in this manner, then Islamic education is concerned with caring for man as a human creation worthy of being presented with knowledge that fulfills his human needs consistent with the origin of his creation, away from the extremism that led its proponents to say that man evolved and originated from a germinal origin, until he became an ape, then a human. And on another side, there is in that an indication of the human nature in terms of the integration of the opposing lines in it, represented by the remote origin and the near origin.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage classifies the origin of man into two types: the remote origin (al-aṣl al-baʿīd) which refers to the beginning of human creation with Adam, and the near origin (al-aṣl al-qarīb) which refers to the embryo's development in the mother's womb. The author then cites Quranic verses: first, Surah An-Nisa 4:1 which speaks of creation from a single soul and its spouse, establishing the human family. A footnote discusses Sayyid Qutb's view that there is no reliable Islamic trace for the creation of Eve from Adam, preferring the interpretation that the single soul represents the unity of male and female in essence. Next, Surah Al-An'am 6:98 is cited, pairing the remote and near origins. The author then addresses the exegetical dispute over the words 'mustaqarr' (place of settlement) and 'mustawdaʿ' (place of deposit), concluding that 'mustaqarr' refers to the womb (female) and 'mustawdaʿ' to the loins (male), as held by Ibn Jarir, Ibn 'Ashur, and Qutb. Finally, the author draws implications for Islamic education: it must care for man as a human creation and provide knowledge consistent with his origin, rejecting evolutionist claims that man descended from apes. The dual origin indicates the integration of opposing lines in human nature.

أصل بعيد (aṣl baʿīd)
أصل قريب (aṣl qarīb)
مستقر (mustaqarr)
مستودع (mustawdaʿ)

The Two Origins of Man in the Quran
Author defines and contrasts distant and near human origins from Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the distant origin (al-asl al-ba'id) and the near origin (al-asl al-qarib) as defined in the Quran.

Core Idea
Man's creation from dust and from a single soul forms two complementary origins.

The passage introduces two key Quranic concepts: the distant origin (creation from dust) and the near origin (creation from a single soul). These are not contradictory but integrated. This lesson will help you define each and understand their relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two origins
Look at the Arabic terms الأصل البعيد and الأصل القريب. Note where they appear in the passage.
2
Distinguish their meanings
From the translation, distant origin refers to creation from dust; near origin refers to creation from one soul and subsequently from sperm.
3
Connect to Quranic verses
The passage quotes 4:1 and 6:98. See how each verse relates to one origin.
4
Understand the integration
The author says the two origins are integrated in human nature. Reflect on how they complement each other.

الباب الثاني: الفصل الثاني- ميدان المعرفة - الغيب والشهادة
وريَا أَيُّهَا النَّاسُ اتَّقُوا رَبَّكُمُ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ مِنْ نَفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ وَخَلَقَ مِنْهَا زَوْجَهَا وَبَثَّ مِنْهُمَا رِجَالاً كَثِيرًا وَنِسَاءً وَاتَّقُوا اللهَ الَّذِي تَسَاءَلُونَ بِهِ وَالأَرْحَامَ إِنَّ اللَّهَ كَانَ عَلَيْكُمْ رَقِيبًا [النساء: ١/٤].
وبالنسبة إلى الأصل القريب فإن القرآن يعمد في إشارته إلى ذلك إلى إقرانه بالأصل البعيد:
﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي أَنْشَأَكُمْ مِنْ نَفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ فَمُسْتَقَرٌّ وَمُسْتَوْدَعْ قَدْ فَصَّلْنَا الآياتِ لِقَوْمٍ يَفْقَهُونَ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٩٨/٦].
وعلى الرغم من الخلاف الدائر بين المفسرين على معنى (المستقر والمستودع) إلا أن اتساع الخلاف لا يخرج عن كون المستقر في الرحم إنما هو بالنسبة إلى الأنثى، والمستودع في الصلب إنما هو بالنسبة للرجل. وكلا المعنيين مشمول بهذه الآية، كما ذهب إلى ذلك ابن جرير (۱) ، وتابعه ابن عاشور (۳) ، ورجح هذا الاختيار سيد قطب (۳).
وإذا عرفنا تقرير القرآن لأصل الإنسان على هذا النحو، فإن التربية الإسلامية معنية بالاهتمام بالإنسان بوصفه مخلوقاً آدمياً يستحق أن تقدم له المعارف الملبية لحاجاته الآدمية الإنسانية المتسقة مع أصل خلقته، بعيداً عن الشطط الذي أوقع أصحابه في القول بتطور الإنسان ونشأته من أصل جرثومي، حتى صار قرداً، ثم إنساناً. ومن جانب آخر فإن في ذلك إشارة إلى الطبيعة الإنسانية من حيث تكامل الخطوط المتقابلة فيها، المتمثلة في الأصل البعيد، والأصل القريب.
(۱) الطبري، المرجع السابق ٢٨٦/٥. وانظر الخلاف حول تفسير الآية، ٢٨١ - ٢٨٦ . (۲) ابن عاشور تفسير التحرير والتنوير، ۳۹۷/۷ (۳) سید قطب، الظلال، ١١٥٩/٢.
Chapter Two, Section Two: The Field of Knowledge – The Unseen and the Witnessed. And O people, fear your Lord who created you from one soul and created from it its mate and dispersed from the two many men and women. And fear Allah, through whom you ask one another, and the wombs. Indeed Allah is ever over you an Observer. [An-Nisa 4:1]. Regarding the near origin, the Quran intends, in its indication to that, to pair it with the distant origin: And it is He who produced you from one soul, and [there is for you] a place of settlement and a place of storage. We have detailed the signs for a people who understand. [Al-An'am 6:98]. Despite the disagreement among commentators on the meaning of 'al-mustaqar' and 'al-mustawda', the broadness of the disagreement does not go beyond that 'al-mustaqar' (the place of settlement) in the womb is relative to the female, and 'al-mustawda' (the place of storage) in the loins is relative to the male. Both meanings are included in this verse, as Ibn Jarir opined, followed by Ibn 'Ashur, and this choice was preferred by Sayyid Qutb. When we know the Quran's affirmation of the origin of man in this manner, Islamic education is concerned with paying attention to man as a human creature deserving to be provided with knowledge that meets his human needs consistent with the origin of his creation, far from the extremism that led its proponents to say that man evolved and originated from a germinal origin, until he became a monkey, then a human. On the other hand, there is in that an indication of human nature in terms of the integration of opposing lines in it, represented by the distant origin and the near origin. (1) Al-Tabari, previous reference 5/286. See the disagreement on interpretation of the verse 281-286. (2) Ibn 'Ashur, Tafsir al-Tahrir wa al-Tanwir, 7/397. (3) Sayyid Qutb, al-Zilal, 2/1159.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing the second section of the second chapter, titled 'The Field of Knowledge – The Unseen and the Witnessed'. It then presents Quran 4:1 which states that all humans are created from a single soul, emphasizing the common origin of humanity. The discussion then turns to the concepts of 'distant origin' (al-asl al-ba'id) and 'near origin' (al-asl al-qarib). The Quran pairs the near origin (creation from a single soul) with the distant origin (creation from dust) by quoting 6:98, which mentions a 'place of settlement' (mustaqar) and a 'place of storage' (mustawda'). The author notes that commentators differ on the meaning of these two terms, but the consensus is that 'mustaqar' refers to the womb of the female and 'mustawda' to the loins of the male. The author cites Ibn Jarir, Ibn 'Ashur, and Sayyid Qutb in support. After establishing the Quranic foundation, the passage explains the educational implications: Islamic education must address man as a human creature, providing knowledge that suits his human needs and original creation, rejecting the theory of evolution that claims humans descended from monkeys. Finally, it highlights the integration of the distant and near origins in human nature.

الأصل البعيد
الأصل القريب

Al-Mustaqar and Al-Mustawda' in Quran 6:98
Exegesis of mustaqar and mustawda' in Q 6:98.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the different interpretations of mustaqar and mustawda' and the consensus view that they refer to the female womb and male loins respectively.

Core Idea
Despite initial disagreement, mufassirun agree that mustaqar is the womb and mustawda' is the loins.

The passage highlights a disagreement among commentators on the meaning of two terms in 6:98. However, the author shows that the disagreement does not go beyond a gender-specific interpretation. This lesson traces the commentary chain.

Guided Path
1
Locate the verse
Find Quran 6:98 in the Arabic: 'فَمُسْتَقَرٌّ وَمُسْتَوْدَع'.
2
Identify the disagreement
The passage says there is disagreement but it is limited. Note the commentators cited: Ibn Jarir, Ibn 'Ashur, Sayyid Qutb.
3
Extract the consensus
The consensus: mustaqar = womb of female; mustawda' = loins of male.
4
Understand the significance
This interpretation connects to the near origin (single soul) and explains biological reproduction.

﴿وَهُوَ الَّذِي أَنْشَأَكُمْ مِنْ نَفْسٍ وَاحِدَةٍ فَمُسْتَقَرٌّ وَمُسْتَوْدَعْ قَدْ فَصَّلْنَا الآياتِ لِقَوْمٍ يَفْقَهُونَ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٩٨/٦].
وعلى الرغم من الخلاف الدائر بين المفسرين على معنى (المستقر والمستودع) إلا أن اتساع الخلاف لا يخرج عن كون المستقر في الرحم إنما هو بالنسبة إلى الأنثى، والمستودع في الصلب إنما هو بالنسبة للرجل. وكلا المعنيين مشمول بهذه الآية، كما ذهب إلى ذلك ابن جرير (۱) ، وتابعه ابن عاشور (۳) ، ورجح هذا الاختيار سيد قطب (۳).
وإذا عرفنا تقرير القرآن لأصل الإنسان على هذا النحو، فإن التربية الإسلامية معنية بالاهتمام بالإنسان بوصفه مخلوقاً آدمياً يستحق أن تقدم له المعارف الملبية لحاجاته الآدمية الإنسانية المتسقة مع أصل خلقته، بعيداً عن الشطط الذي أوقع أصحابه في القول بتطور الإنسان ونشأته من أصل جرثومي، حتى صار قرداً، ثم إنساناً. ومن جانب آخر فإن في ذلك إشارة إلى الطبيعة الإنسانية من حيث تكامل الخطوط المتقابلة فيها، المتمثلة في الأصل البعيد، والأصل القريب.
(۱) الطبري، المرجع السابق ٢٨٦/٥. وانظر الخلاف حول تفسير الآية، ٢٨١ - ٢٨٦ . (۲) ابن عاشور تفسير التحرير والتنوير، ۳۹۷/۷ (۳) سید قطب، الظلال، ١١٥٩/٢.
Chapter Two, Section Two: The Field of Knowledge – The Unseen and the Witnessed. And O people, fear your Lord who created you from one soul and created from it its mate and dispersed from the two many men and women. And fear Allah, through whom you ask one another, and the wombs. Indeed Allah is ever over you an Observer. [An-Nisa 4:1]. Regarding the near origin, the Quran intends, in its indication to that, to pair it with the distant origin: And it is He who produced you from one soul, and [there is for you] a place of settlement and a place of storage. We have detailed the signs for a people who understand. [Al-An'am 6:98]. Despite the disagreement among commentators on the meaning of 'al-mustaqar' and 'al-mustawda', the broadness of the disagreement does not go beyond that 'al-mustaqar' (the place of settlement) in the womb is relative to the female, and 'al-mustawda' (the place of storage) in the loins is relative to the male. Both meanings are included in this verse, as Ibn Jarir opined, followed by Ibn 'Ashur, and this choice was preferred by Sayyid Qutb. When we know the Quran's affirmation of the origin of man in this manner, Islamic education is concerned with paying attention to man as a human creature deserving to be provided with knowledge that meets his human needs consistent with the origin of his creation, far from the extremism that led its proponents to say that man evolved and originated from a germinal origin, until he became a monkey, then a human. On the other hand, there is in that an indication of human nature in terms of the integration of opposing lines in it, represented by the distant origin and the near origin. (1) Al-Tabari, previous reference 5/286. See the disagreement on interpretation of the verse 281-286. (2) Ibn 'Ashur, Tafsir al-Tahrir wa al-Tanwir, 7/397. (3) Sayyid Qutb, al-Zilal, 2/1159.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing the second section of the second chapter, titled 'The Field of Knowledge – The Unseen and the Witnessed'. It then presents Quran 4:1 which states that all humans are created from a single soul, emphasizing the common origin of humanity. The discussion then turns to the concepts of 'distant origin' (al-asl al-ba'id) and 'near origin' (al-asl al-qarib). The Quran pairs the near origin (creation from a single soul) with the distant origin (creation from dust) by quoting 6:98, which mentions a 'place of settlement' (mustaqar) and a 'place of storage' (mustawda'). The author notes that commentators differ on the meaning of these two terms, but the consensus is that 'mustaqar' refers to the womb of the female and 'mustawda' to the loins of the male. The author cites Ibn Jarir, Ibn 'Ashur, and Sayyid Qutb in support. After establishing the Quranic foundation, the passage explains the educational implications: Islamic education must address man as a human creature, providing knowledge that suits his human needs and original creation, rejecting the theory of evolution that claims humans descended from monkeys. Finally, it highlights the integration of the distant and near origins in human nature.

The Quranic Stages of Human Creation
Author enumerates the stages of human creation from Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the major stages of human creation as described in the Quran and explain how this sequence demonstrates Allah's creative power.

Core Idea
The Quranic stages form a detailed chronological proof of Allah's power and wisdom.

The Quran does not merely mention creation in passing; it provides a precise, step-by-step sequence from dust to full human life. This lesson guides you through the key verses and their logical order, showing how each stage points to the Creator's design. You will learn to identify the stages and understand their cumulative argument for tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the starting point
Begin with verse 40:67 ('He created you from dust, then from a sperm-drop...'). Note that the very first material is dust (turab), though earlier creation from nothing (Maryam 19:67) establishes absolute origination.
2
List the stages from Ghafir 40:67
Extract the sequence: dust → sperm-drop (nutfa) → clinging clot ('alaqa) → child (tifl) → maturity (ashudd) → old age (shuyukh). Recognize that this verse covers the entire lifespan from raw material to old age.
The verse also notes that some die before reaching old age.
3
Add details from Al-Mu'minun 23:12-14
This verse provides a finer ontology: clay extract (sulala min tin) → sperm-drop → clot → lump (mudgha) → bones → flesh → new creation. Compare with the previous list to see how the embryonic stages are made explicit.
The 'new creation' (khalqan akhar) refers to ensoulment or distinct human form.
4
Observe the pattern in Ar-Rum 30:54
This verse summarizes the entire process as a progression from weakness (da'f) to strength (quwwa) back to weakness. Identify which stages correspond to each: embryonic/childhood = first weakness, youth = strength, old age = final weakness with grey hair.
The verse emphasizes Allah's wisdom and power in this cycle.
5
Conclude the purpose
The passage states that these different expressions ultimately prove God's power over innovative creation (al-khalq al-mubdi'). Recognize that the logical sequence serves as an argument (istidlal) for tawhid and resurrection.

وأنه قد مر بمراحل عدة حتى بلغ طوره الرفيع

والآية تصور بدقة المراحل الرئيسة في خلق الإنسان، التي تبدأ من المرحلة الترابية بما يعني تذكيراً بأول الخلق في أصله البعيد، ثم أول المواد وهو المني في الأصل القريب أو أن ذلك إشارة إلى خلق الإنسان من عدة أغذية متولدة من التراب (۱).

ولخص قصة الخلق فقال:
اللهُ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ مِنْ ضَعْفِ ثُمَّ جَعَلَ مِنْ بَعْدِ ضَعْفِ قُوَّةً ثُمَّ جَعَلَ مِنْ بَعْدِ قُوَّةٍ ضَعْفًا وَشَيْبَةٌ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَهُوَ الْعَلِيمُ الْقَدِيرُ﴾ [الروم: ٥٤/٣٠]. إن هذه الآية الأخيرة تصف مراحل الإنسان وتقسمها إلى مرحلة الضعف الجنينية والطفولة، وضعف الشيخوخة ومرحلة القوة بينهما وهي مرحلة الشباب.
(۱) محمد جمال الدين القاسمي، تفسير القاسمي المسمى محاسن التأويل، المجلد السابع، ۸/۱۲، والمجلد الثامن، ٢٤٨/١٤.
Stages of Creation. The Noble Quran declares that man was created from nothing. Does man not remember that We created him before, while he was nothing? [Maryam 19:67]. And that he passed through several stages until he reached his high state. It is He who created you from dust, then from a sperm-drop, then from a clinging clot, then He brings you out as a child, then [He lets you] reach your maturity, then [He lets you] become old, and among you are some who are taken before, so that you may reach an appointed term, and so that you may understand. [Ghafir 40:67]. And the verse accurately depicts the main stages of the creation of man, which begin from the stage of earth, meaning a reminder of the first creation in his distant origin, then the first material which is sperm in the near origin, or that it is an indication that man is created from several nutrients generated from earth (1). The expression of these stages has come in different forms, but they ultimately lead to proving God's power over innovative creation, including: And We created man from an extract of clay. Then We placed him as a sperm-drop in a firm lodging. Then We made the sperm-drop into a clinging clot, and We made the clot into a lump, and We made the lump into bones, and We clothed the bones with flesh; then We brought him forth as another creation. So blessed is Allah, the best of creators. [Al-Mu'minun 23:12-14]. And He summarized the story of creation, saying: Allah is He who created you from weakness, then made after weakness strength, then made after strength weakness and white hair. He creates what He wills, and He is the Knowing, the Competent. [Ar-Rum 30:54]. This last verse describes the stages of man and divides them into the stage of embryonic weakness and childhood, the weakness of old age, and the stage of strength between them, which is the stage of youth. (1) Muhammad Jamal al-Din al-Qasimi, Tafsir al-Qasimi known as Mahasin al-Ta'wil, vol. 7, p. 12/8, and vol. 8, p. 14/248.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and expository section titled 'Stages of Creation'. It begins by asserting that the Quran declares man was created from nothing, citing verse 19:67. Then it presents a key verse (40:67) that outlines a sequence: dust, then sperm-drop, then clinging clot, then child, then maturity, then old age. The commentary explains that this verse accurately depicts the main stages, starting from the earthy stage (reminding of the distant origin), then the first material (sperm in the near origin), or alternatively that it refers to man being created from nutrients derived from earth. The author then notes that different Quranic expressions of these stages ultimately aim to prove God's power over innovative creation. Another detailed verse (23:12-14) is cited: from clay extract to sperm-drop, clot, lump (mudgha), bones, flesh, then another creation. Finally, a summary verse (30:54) is given, dividing life into three phases: prenatal/childhood weakness, youthful strength, and old-age weakness. The passage explains that this last verse categorizes human stages into embryonic and childhood weakness, strength (youth), and senility weakness.

Stages of Human Creation: Qur'anic Foundations for Gradual Education
Author describes Quranic creation stages and educational implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic stages of human creation and explain their implications for curriculum design (content and methods).

Core Idea
The Quran's description of creation stages teaches that education must be gradual and developmentally appropriate.

The Quran does not merely narrate the stages of human creation as biological facts; it presents them in multiple forms to emphasize different aspects. The author of our passage draws out a crucial educational lesson: just as human development is gradual, so too must education be gradual in content and method. This lesson will help you see how Quranic verses can directly inform pedagogical theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key stages mentioned in the Quran
Read the verses cited: Maryam 19:67, Ghafir 40:67, Al-Mu'minun 23:12-14, and Ar-Rum 30:54. List the stages in order. Note which stages are repeated across verses and which are unique.
The stages include: nothingness, dust, sperm-drop, clinging clot, lump of flesh, bones, flesh, then another creation; also weakness, strength, weakness/shayba.
2
Analyze the purpose of multiple descriptions
Why does the Quran describe the same process in different ways? The author says the different expressions 'ultimately lead to proving God's power of innovative creation.' Consider how each description highlights a different aspect of divine power or human dependence.
For example, the dust-stage connects to the original creation, while the sperm-drop emphasizes the proximate origin.
3
Extract the educational principles
The author states: 'Content must take into account the stages of the learner's development... methods must include the method of gradualness.' Identify the two elements of the curriculum the author mentions and explain how they relate to the stages described.
The two elements are content (what is taught) and methods (how it is taught). Gradualness is key.
4
Connect to curriculum theory
Reflect on how the principle of gradualness from creation can be applied to organizing knowledge in any Islamic subject. The author says it benefits 'organizing knowledge and arranging it logically.' Try to think of a fiqh topic and how it might be taught step-by-step.
For instance, in teaching fiqh, one might start with purification, then prayer, then fasting, etc., mirroring the gradual revelation of rulings.

yصرح القرآن الكريم بأن الإنسان خلق من عدم.

وأنه قد مر بمراحل عدة حتى بلغ طوره الرفيع
هُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ مِنْ تُرَابٍ ثُمَّ مِنْ نُطْفَةٍ ثُمَّ مِنْ عَلَقَةٍ ثُمَّ يُخْرِجُكُمْ طِفلاً ثُمَّ لِتَبْلُغُوا أَشُدَّكُمْ ثُمَّ لِتَكُونُوا شُيُوخاً وَمِنْكُمْ مَنْ يُتَوَفَّى مِنْ قَبْلُ وَلِتَبْلُغُوا أَجَلاً مُسَمَّى وَلَعَلَّكُمْ تَعْقِلُونَ [غافر: ٦٧/٤٠].

ولخص قصة الخلق فقال:
اللهُ الَّذِي خَلَقَكُمْ مِنْ ضَعْفٍ ثُمَّ جَعَلَ مِنْ بَعْدِ ضَعْفٍ قُوَّةً ثُمَّ جَعَلَ مِنْ بَعْدِ قُوَّةٍ ضَعْفًا وَشَيْبَةً يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَهُوَ الْعَلِيمُ الْقَدِيرُ [الروم: ٥٤/٣٠]. إن هذه الآية الأخيرة تصف مراحل الإنسان وتقسمها إلى مرحلة الضعف الجنينية والطفولة، وضعف الشيخوخة ومرحلة القوة بينهما وهي مرحلة الشباب.
ونستخلص مما سبق تقرير القرآن لمراحل النمو عند الإنسان، وهو ما يفيدنا في عنصري المحتوى والطرائق من المنهج. فالمحتوى يجب أن يكون مراعياً لمراحل نمو المتعلم، بحيث يقدّم له المعارف المناسبة لسنه ومستوى إدراكه. والطرائق يجب أن تشمل أسلوب التدرّج في تقديم المعرفة للمتعلم كذلك، بحيث تلتزم بهذا الأسلوب، كلما استدعى الموقف التعليمي التربوي مراعاته واستخدامه. وفوق هذا فإن ذلك يفيدنا في عملية تنظيم المعرفة وترتيبها منطقياً.
Stages of creation: The Noble Quran declares that man was created from nothing. 'Or does man not remember that We created him before, while he was nothing?' [Maryam: 19:67]. And that he passed through several stages until he reached his high level. 'It is He who created you from dust, then from a sperm-drop, then from a clinging clot, then He brings you out as a child, then [He develops you] that you reach your [time of] maturity, then [further] that you become elders. And among you is he who is taken in death before [that], so that you reach a specified term, and perhaps you will use reason.' [Ghafir: 40:67]. And the verse accurately depicts the main stages of human creation, which begin from the dust stage, meaning a reminder of the first creation in its distant origin, then the first material which is the semen in the near origin, or that is an indication of the creation of man from several nutrients generated from dust (1). And the expression of these stages came in different forms, but they ultimately lead to proving God's power of innovative creation, including: 'And We certainly created man from an extract of clay. Then We placed him as a sperm-drop in a firm lodging. Then We made the sperm-drop into a clinging clot, and We made the clot into a lump [of flesh], and We made the lump into bones, and We covered the bones with flesh; then We developed him into another creation. So blessed is Allah, the best of creators.' [Al-Mu'minun: 23:12-14]. And he summarized the story of creation, saying: 'Allah is the one who created you from weakness, then made after weakness strength, then made after strength weakness and white hair. He creates what He wills, and He is the Knowing, the Competent.' [Ar-Rum: 30:54]. Indeed, this last verse describes the stages of man and divides them into the stage of fetal weakness and childhood, the weakness of old age, and the stage of strength between them, which is the stage of youth. And we conclude from the above the Qur'an's determination of the stages of human development, which benefits us in the two elements of content and methods of the curriculum. So content must take into account the stages of the learner's development, providing him with knowledge appropriate to his age and level of perception. And methods must include the method of gradualness in presenting knowledge to the learner as well, adhering to this method whenever the educational situation requires its observation and use. Moreover, this benefits us in the process of organizing knowledge and arranging it logically.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage enumerates the Quranic verses describing the stages of human creation from nothingness to old age. The author first cites verses that emphasize creation from nothing (Maryam), then the sequential stages from dust to youth to old age (Ghafir). He explains that the dust stage refers to the distant origin of man, while the sperm stage is the proximate origin. Then he cites the detailed sequence from clay to sperm to clot to lump to bones to flesh to another creation (Al-Mu'minun), and finally the summary verse from Ar-Rum that divides life into weakness, strength, and weakness again. The author concludes by drawing educational implications: the curriculum's content and methods must be gradual, tailored to the learner's developmental stage, and the organization of knowledge should follow a logical sequence.

Man’s Unique Cognitive Position: Vicegerency Through Knowledge
Author describes man's unique cognitive capacity and his role as vicegerent over creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of man's cognitive vicegerency (al-qayyumiyya al-ma'rifiyya) and its foundation in the Quranic story of Adam.

Core Idea
Man's cognitive capacity to internalize the universe makes him God's vicegerent on earth.

This passage from a contemporary Islamic epistemology text sets out a powerful vision of man's place in creation. The author argues that man's unique cognitive abilities — the ability to comprehend beings in their quantitative and qualitative aspects — are what qualify him to be God's representative (khalifa) over creation. This is not merely a spiritual station but an epistemic one grounded in the Quranic account of Adam being taught the names.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key capacities
Locate in the Arabic the phrases 'الاستيعاب المعرفي للكائنات' (cognitive comprehension of beings) and 'القيومية والإشراف' (guardianship and supervision). What do these refer to? The first describes man's ability to grasp the external world; the second describes the resulting authority over creation.
2
Understand the mechanism
How does the author explain the process of transferring the external world to the internal world? He says man's cognitive perceptual means (وسائله المعرفية الإدراكية) enable him to transfer the world in its quantitative and qualitative form (صورته الكمية والكيفية) to his internal world. This makes him a 'carrier' (حاملاً) of the world.
3
Find the Quranic evidence
Read the ayah cited (Quran 2:31-32): 'And He taught Adam the names, all of them...' How does this story support the author's claim? Adam's knowledge of the names demonstrates his comprehensive cognitive capacity, surpassing even the angels, thereby establishing his position of authority.
4
Trace the implication
What does this imply for man's role in the Islamic worldview? Relate it to the concept of khilafa (vicegerency). The author explicitly uses the term 'قيومية' (guardianship), which echoes the idea of man as Allah's deputy on earth, entrusted with knowledge and responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موقع الإنسان
yحتل الإنسان موقع القطبية المتفردة في هذا الوجود على مستوى المعرفة. وذلك لما خص به من قدرة على الاستيعاب المعرفي للكائنات، حيث إن وسائله المعرفية الإدراكية تهيؤه لنقل العالم الخارجي في صورته الكمية والكيفية إلى عالمه الداخلي. وبذلك فإن هذا الكائن الصغير يصبح حاملاً في ذاته لهذا العالم الكبير، إذ يكون قد حصل على القيومية والإشراف على سائر الكائنات، وذلك ضرب من ضروب الرفعة والاستعلاء. وجاء التأكيد عليها في قصة خلق آدم: وَعَلَّمَ آدَمَ الْأَسْمَاءَ كُلَّهَا ثُمَّ عَرَضَهُمْ عَلَى الْمَلَائِكَةِ فَقَالَ أَنْبِئُونِي بِأَسْمَاءِ هَؤُلَاءِ إِنْ كُنْتُمْ صَادِقِينَ، قَالُوا سُبْحَانَكَ لَا عِلْمَ لَنَا إِلَّا مَا عَلَّمْتَنَا إِنَّكَ أَنْتَ الْعَلِيمُ الْحَكِيمُ [البقرة: ۳۱/۲-۳۲] (۱).
ووفقاً لذلك فإن الإنسان يمتاز بجملة خصائص يمكن أن تنطوي تحت هاتين الخصلتين الرئيستين:
(۱) عبد المجيد النجار، مباحث في منهجية الفكر الإسلامي، ٢٣.
Man's Position: Man occupies a unique polar position in this existence at the level of knowledge. That is because of the capacity he is endowed with for cognitive comprehension of beings, as his cognitive perceptual means prepare him to transfer the external world in its quantitative and qualitative form to his internal world. Thus this small being becomes in himself a carrier of this great world, having attained guardianship and supervision over all other beings, which is a type of elevation and superiority. Emphasis on this came in the story of Adam's creation: 'And He taught Adam the names, all of them; then He presented them to the angels and said: Inform Me of the names of these, if you are truthful. They said: Exalted are You; we have no knowledge except what You have taught us. Indeed, it is You who is the Knowing, the Wise.' [Al-Baqarah: 2:31-32] (1). Accordingly, man is distinguished by a set of characteristics that can be subsumed under these two main traits: (1) Abdul Majeed al-Najjar, Mabahith fi Manhajiyyat al-Fikr al-Islami, p. 23.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage defines man's unique epistemic position in the universe. The author argues that man is endowed with perceptual means that allow him to internalize the external world in both quantitative and qualitative aspects, making him a microcosm bearing the macrocosm and giving him authority over other beings. This is supported by the Quranic story of Adam being taught the names, demonstrating man's capacity for comprehensive knowledge. The author then notes that man's characteristics stem from these two main traits (the text is cut off, but likely continues).

The Quran's Exclusive Focus on Sunan al-Anfus
Author explains that Quranic sunan are exclusively related to the realm of the selves, not the horizons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Quranic concept of sunan from natural laws and identify its exclusive domain as the realm of the selves (anfus).

Core Idea
Quranic sunan are about human psyche and society, not physical horizons.

This passage highlights a key distinction: when the Quran mentions sunan (divine laws/patterns), it always refers to the psychological and social realm, not the physical cosmos. This challenges common assumptions and shapes how we approach Quranic exegesis and understanding of divine order. The author emphasizes that the Quran's focus is on the realm of the selves (anfus), not the horizons (afaq), and warns against mistakenly belittling human ability in the name of God's greatness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find the author's main assertion about where the Quran mentions sunan. The passage states that the Quran does not go beyond the psychological and social laws in the field of the selves into the field of the horizons.
Look for the phrase 'لا يتعدى بها السنن النفسية والاجتماعية في ميدان الأنفس إلى ميدان الآفاق'.
2
Find supporting evidence
The author argues that a contextual analysis reveals no mention of sunna in the Quran except linked to the selves. This is the key evidence: 'لا يعثر على ذكر ألبتة للسنة في القرآن إلا مرتبطة بميدان الأنفس فحسب'.
3
Understand the rationale
The Quran pays attention to the mind to free it from absurd beliefs and to perceive order in the universe, as shown by the quoted verses (44:38 and 23:115). This indicates the purpose of sunan is to develop human insight and awareness, not to describe physical laws.
The rationale is epistemological: sunan help humans understand their purpose and avoid futility.
4
Recognize the implication for human ability
The author refutes the mistaken view that belittling human ability preserves God's greatness. Instead, human ability is a gift from God and does not diminish His majesty. This follows from understanding sunan: 'زيادة سلطانه تقلل من مشيئة الله وعظمته' actually a false notion.
This implication is crucial for proper tawhid: God's majesty is shown by granting capabilities.
5
Summarize the importance
The passage concludes that benefiting from sunan provides insight, helps avoid past mistakes, and gives firmness of stance. The stance of one who perceives sunan differs from one ignorant of the source of events.
This shows practical importance for personal and societal development.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سنن الأنفس
يتناول القرآن الكريم سنن عالم الأنفس مشيراً إلى أهميتها، وأبرز سماتها، ومظاهرها المتنوعة، ويمكن تسليط الضوء على ذلك كله فيما يلي:
أهمية السنن
مما يستحق الإشارة هنا إدراك أن القرآن الكريم إذ يتناول السنن فإنه لا يتعدى بها السنن النفسية والاجتماعية في ميدان الأنفس إلى ميدان الآفاق حتى إن الباحث لا يعثر على ذكر ألبتة للسنة في القرآن إلا مرتبطة بميدان الأنفس فحسب، إذا ما أخذ معيار السياق.
وثمة حقيقة لا يجوز التعامي عنها، وهي أن القرآن الكريم إذ يهتم بالعقل كي يظل مشتعلاً مدركاً لسنن الله فإنما يعمل على تخليصه من العقيدة العبثية التي تصيبه بالعطالة:
﴿وَما خَلَقْنَا السَّماواتِ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُما لاعبين ﴾ [الدخان: ٣٨/٤٤].
أَفَحَسِبْتُمْ أَنما خَلَقْنَاكُمْ عَبَثًا﴾ [المؤمنون: ١١٥/٢٣].
إن ذلك يعني عدم إدراك النظام والسنن في هذا الكون، وعدم القدرة على الربط بين علاقة الطاقة المفكرة عند الإنسان وسنن الله في الوجود.
إن التصور الخاطئ الذي قد يقع فيه بعض المسلمين حين يميلون إلى الحط من قدرة الإنسان لتبقى مشيئة الله وعظمته يعني أن عجز الإنسان يثبت مشيئة الله وعظمته. ولو تم قدر من التأمل - في مسألة السنن - لما شعر أحد بأن زيادة سلطانه تقلل من مشيئة الله وعظمته تعالى، بل إن من جلال الله - سبحانه أن يمنح عبده الإنسان هذه القدرات.
-وبوسع المرء أن يلخص أهمية السنن بالقول: إن الاستفادة من السنن وملاحظة الأمثلة والأحداث، تقدم للناس بصراً ومعرفة نظرية وعملية، حتى لا يقعوا فيما وقع فيه من قبلهم أو تنقذهم إذا وقعوا فيها، أو على أقل تقدير تكسبهم صلابة موقف من يدرك السنة، لأن موقف من يرى السنن يختلف عن نظر وموقف من يجهل مصدر الأحداث.
The Laws of the Selves. The Noble Qur'an deals with the laws of the world of the selves, pointing to their importance, their most prominent features, and their various manifestations. Light can be shed on all of that as follows: The Importance of the Laws. It is worth noting here to realize that when the Noble Qur'an deals with laws, it does not go beyond them into psychological and social laws in the field of the selves into the field of the horizons, so that the researcher never finds any mention of sunna in the Qur'an except linked to the field of the selves alone, if the criterion of context is taken. And there is a truth that cannot be ignored: that when the Noble Qur'an pays attention to the mind so that it remains ignited and aware of God's laws, it works to free it from the absurd belief that afflicts it with inactivity: 'And We did not create the heavens and the earth and what is between them in play' [al-Dukhan 44:38]. 'Did you think that We created you in vain?' [al-Mu'minun 23:115]. That means not perceiving the order and laws in this universe, and the inability to link the relationship of the thinking energy in man with God's laws in existence. The mistaken conception that may occur to some Muslims when they tend to belittle human ability to preserve God's will and greatness means that human inability proves God's will and greatness. And if there were a measure of reflection on the matter of laws, no one would feel that increasing his authority reduces God's will and greatness, exalted is He; rather, it is part of God's majesty – glorified is He – that He grants His servant man these abilities. And one can summarize the importance of laws by saying: Benefiting from laws and observing examples and events provide people with insight and theoretical and practical knowledge, so that they do not fall into what those before them fell into, or rescue them if they fall into them, or at the very least gain them the firmness of the stance of one who perceives the sunna, because the stance of one who sees the laws differs from the view and stance of one who is ignorant of the source of events.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of sunan (laws) specifically as they appear in the Quran. It begins by stating that the Quran deals with the laws of the world of the selves (anfus), emphasizing their importance and characteristics. The key claim is that the Quran's mention of sunan is exclusively tied to the psychological and social realm, not the physical horizons. This is supported by contextual analysis: researchers find no mention of sunan in the Quran except in relation to the selves. The author then warns against a mistaken view: belittling human ability to preserve God's greatness. This view is refuted; human ability is actually a gift from God and does not diminish His majesty. The passage concludes by summarizing the importance of understanding sunan: they provide insight and practical knowledge, help avoid mistakes of past peoples, and grant firmness of stance. This is an epistemological discussion about the nature of Quranic laws.

Key Terms
السنن (al-sunan): Divine laws or patterns (singular: sunna) that govern the realm of the selves and society, as mentioned in the Quran. [Note: In broader Islamic usage, sunna can also refer to prophetic traditions; here it specifically means natural/social laws.]
الأنفس (al-anfus): The realm of the selves, i.e., human psyche and society, as opposed to the physical horizons. [Note: In Sufi contexts, anfus often refers to the inner self; here it is more sociological.]
الآفاق (al-afaq): The horizons, i.e., the external physical world and cosmos. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In the field of the horizons (al-afaq) only.
B) In both the horizons and the selves.
C) Only in the field of the selves (al-anfus).
D) Only in historical narratives.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's sunan are exclusively about the selves, providing a framework for human society and psychology, not physical nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Quranic Concept of Sunan (Divine Laws)
Author defines sunan as God's laws in the universe, connected to human intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic usage of 'sunnah' and explain its epistemological significance as a pattern of divine laws in the universe.

Core Idea
Sunnah in the Quran refers to God's consistent patterns, not prophetic tradition, and is linked to human intellect and historical observation.

This passage from a modern Quranic epistemology work clarifies a key term: 'sunnah' (or 'sunan') as it appears in the Quran. Unlike the common usage referring to prophetic traditions, the Quran uses 'sunnah' to denote God's established laws and practices in the universe, especially concerning human societies. Understanding this concept is crucial for grasping the Quranic worldview that ties cognitive awareness to divine order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'sunnah' in context
Notice that the passage states the word 'sunnah' appears in the Quran only in connection with the field of the self (anfus). This narrows its meaning to human-related patterns.
The term 'anfus' here refers to the human self or realm, not external nature.
2
Connect to Quranic verses
Examine the cited verses (44:38 and 23:115) which deny purposeless creation. They imply that recognizing a divine order (sunan) is essential to avoid absurdity.
3
Analyze the epistemological claim
The Quran's concern with the intellect is to free it from 'absurd creed' by making it aware of sunan. This links cognitive observation to divine patterns, forming a basis for knowledge.
The passage argues that ignoring sunan leads to inability to perceive order.
4
Distinguish from common usage
Contrast this Quranic meaning with the common Islamic usage of 'sunnah' as prophetic traditions. Here it is a cosmic law, not a human practice.
This distinction is critical for understanding the passage's epistemology.

الباب الثاني: الفصل الثاني- ميدان المعرفة - الغيب والشهادة
إن الباحث لا يعثر على ذكر ألبتة للسنة في القرآن إلا مرتبطة بميدان الأنفس فحسب، إذا ما أخذ معيار السياق (۱).
وثمة حقيقة لا يجوز التعامي عنها، وهي أن القرآن الكريم إذ يهتم بالعقل كي يظل مشتعلاً مدركاً لسنن الله فإنما يعمل على تخليصـه مـن العقيدة العبثية التي تصيبه بالعطالة:
﴿وَما خَلَقْنَا السَّماواتِ وَالْأَرْضَ وَمَا بَيْنَهُما لاعبين ﴾ [الدخان: ٣٨/٤٤].
أَفَحَسِبْتُمْ أَنما خَلَقْنَاكُمْ عَبَشام﴾ [المؤمنون: ١١٥/٢٣].
إن ذلك
يعني عدم إدراك النظام والسنن في هذا الكون، وعدم القدرة على
الربط بين علاقة الطاقة المفكرة عند الإنسان وسنن الله في الوجود (۲).
إن التصور الخاطئ الذي قد يقع فيه بعض المسلمين حين يميلون إلى الحط من قدرة الإنسان لتبقى مشيئة الله وعظمته يعني أن عجز الإنسان يثبت مشيئة الله وعظمته. ولو تم قدر من التأمل - في مسألة السنن - لما شعر أحد بأن زيادة سلطانه تقلل من مشيئة الله وعظمته تعالى، بل إن من جلال الله - سبحانه أن يمنح عبده الإنسان هذه القدرات (۳).

(۱) محمد فؤاد عبد الباقي، المعجم المفهرس لألفاظ القرآن الكريم، ٤٦٥ - ٤٦٦ (مادة سنن). (۲) جودت سعيد، حتى يغيروا ما بأنفسهم، ١٦٤ .

الباب الثاني: الفصل الثاني- ميدان المعرفة - الغيب والشهادة
۱۷۳
ونظراً لما تحمله السنن من أهمية فإن التربية الإسلامية لا تتجاهل منطق السنن، بل تعتمدها مرتكزاً من مرتكزاتها، ففي ضوئها يصمم المنهج التربوي بجميع عناصره، ومن خلالها يقوم المعلم بتفسير الظواهر والأحداث المختلفة التي يعرض لها المنهاج أثناء قيامه بمهمة التدريس، وعلى هـدى مـن الإيمان بأهمية السنن تتضافر جهود أطراف العملية التربوية لإخراج المتعلم المؤمن بفاعلية السنن وآثارها في الفرد والمجتمع وفي الكون كله، والبعيد عن التفكيرات الخرافية القائمة على العشوائية وردود الأفعال المتسرعة.
وقد حدّد المؤتمر العالمي الأول للتعليم الإسلامي المنعقد بمكة المكرمة عام ۱۳۹۷هـ - ۱۹۷۷م أن هدف التعليم الإسلامي هو تنشئة الإنسان الصالح الذي يعبد الله حق عبادته ويعمر الأرض وفق شريعته، ويسخرها لخدمة العقيدة وفق منهجه (۱).
وذلك يعني أن يحقق التعليم أمرين:
أحدهما: يعرف الإنسان بربه، ليعبده اعتقاداً بوحدانيته وأداء لشعائر عبادته، وتطبيقاً لشريعته، والتزاماً لمنهجه.
والثاني: تعريفه لسنن الله في الكون، ليعبـده بعمارة الأرض، والمشي في مناكبها، وتسخير ما خلق الله فيها لحماية العقيدة والتمكين لدينه في الأرض،
امتثالاً لقوله تعالى:
(۲)
هُوَ أَنْشَأَكُمْ مِنَ الأَرْضِ وَاسْتَعْمَرَكُمْ فِيها ﴾ [هود: ٦١/١١) (٢).
(۱) عبد الرحمن الميداني، توصيات المؤتمر الأول للتعليم الإسلامي، ٢٢٥.
(۲) المرجع السابق، ٢٢٦ .
Chapter Two: Section Two - The Field of Knowledge - The Unseen and the Witnessed. The researcher does not find any mention at all of 'sunnah' in the Quran except in connection with the field of the self, if the criterion of context is taken. And there is a truth that cannot be overlooked: that the Noble Quran, when it concerns itself with the intellect so that it remains ignited and aware of God's laws, works to free it from the absurd creed that afflicts it with idleness: {And We did not create the heavens and the earth and what is between them in play} [Ad-Dukhan 44:38]. {Did you think that We created you aimlessly?} [Al-Mu'minun 23:115]. That means not perceiving the order and laws in this universe, and not being able to connect the relationship between the thinking energy in man and God's laws in existence. The mistaken conception that may occur to some Muslims when they tend to belittle man's capacity to preserve God's will and greatness means that man's incapacity proves God's will and greatness. If a measure of reflection were given - on the issue of laws - no one would feel that increasing his authority reduces God's will and greatness, rather it is part of God's majesty - glorified is He - to grant His servant man these capacities. And one can summarize the importance of laws: Indeed, benefiting from laws and observing examples and events provide people with insight and theoretical and practical knowledge, so that they do not fall into what those before them fell into, or rescue them if they fall into them, or at the very least give them firmness of position of one who perceives the law, because the position of one who sees laws differs from the view and position of one who is ignorant of the source of events. (Footnotes) Given the importance of laws, Islamic education does not ignore the logic of laws, but adopts them as one of its foundations; in their light the educational curriculum is designed with all its elements, and through them the teacher explains the various phenomena and events that the curriculum presents while he performs the task of teaching. Guided by faith in the importance of laws, the efforts of all parties in the educational process combine to produce the learner who believes in the effectiveness of laws and their effects on the individual, society, and the entire universe, and who is far from superstitious thoughts based on randomness and hasty reactions. The first World Conference on Islamic Education held in Mecca in 1397 AH - 1977 CE specified that the goal of Islamic education is to nurture the righteous human being who worships God truly and cultivates the earth according to His Sharia, and harnesses it to serve the faith according to His methodology. That means that education must achieve two things: One: making man know his Lord, to worship Him by believing in His oneness, performing the rituals of His worship, applying His Sharia, and adhering to His methodology. And the second: acquainting him with God's laws in the universe, to worship Him by cultivating the earth, walking in its pathways, and harnessing what God created in it to protect the faith and empower His religion on earth, in compliance with the verse: {He brought you forth from the earth and made you its cultivators} [Hud 11:61].
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion from a modern Quranic perspective, not a classical logic text. It defines the concept of 'sunnah' (divine laws/practices) as it appears in the Quran, linking it to the field of the self (anfus) rather than external phenomena. The author argues that the Quran's focus on the intellect is to free it from absurd beliefs that lead to inertia, and that recognizing God's laws (sunan) is essential for understanding the order in the universe and connecting human cognitive power to divine patterns. The text then addresses a misconception: that belittling human capacity exalts God's will. Instead, it asserts that God's majesty is shown by granting humans abilities. The passage further elaborates on the importance of sunan in providing insight and preventing past mistakes, and then relates this to Islamic education, stating that education must be based on the logic of sunan. The ultimate goal is to produce a learner who worships God through both belief and action, including the cultivation of the earth according to God's laws. The passage cites Qur'anic verses and conference resolutions to support its points. Although not a classical logic text, it engages with epistemological themes relevant to Islamic thought.

The Quranic concept of sunan refers to God's consistent laws in the universe, which the intellect must recognize to avoid absurdity.

Sunan as a Foundation for Islamic Education
Author argues that Islamic education is rooted in the logic of divine laws (sunan).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the concept of sunan is applied as a foundational principle in Islamic education according to the passage.

Core Idea
Islamic education must be built on the logic of sunan to produce believers who worship God through both belief and action in the world.

This passage extends the epistemological concept of sunan into practical education. It argues that Islamic education cannot ignore the logic of divine laws; instead, the curriculum, teaching methods, and overall goals must be designed in light of sunan. The ultimate aim is to nurture a learner who understands God's patterns and applies them to cultivate the earth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational claim
The passage asserts that Islamic education does not ignore the logic of sunan but adopts it as a foundation (مرتكز). This is a key statement about the integration of Quranic epistemology into pedagogy.
2
See how sunan shapes curriculum and teaching
The curriculum is designed in light of sunan, and the teacher uses sunan to explain phenomena and events. This shows practical application in educational design.
3
Understand the goal of education
The passage describes producing a learner who believes in the effectiveness of sunan and is free from superstitious thoughts. This is the outcome of an education rooted in divine laws.
4
Examine the two objectives from the Mecca conference
The conference defined two objectives: knowing the Lord (belief) and knowing His sunan (action in the world). This reflects the integration of faith and worldly cultivation.

Universality and Constancy of Divine Patterns
Author explains universality and constancy of divine patterns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and support the universality and constancy of divine patterns (sunan) using Quranic evidence and historical examples.

Core Idea
Divine patterns are universal and unchanging, applicable to all humans without exception.

The author emphasizes that the most prominent feature of divine patterns (sunan) is their consistency (ittiraad) and universality (umoom). These patterns are not limited to believers but encompass all of humanity, as evidenced by Quranic verses and historical examples. Understanding this is crucial for a correct Islamic worldview and avoiding the error of thinking Muslims are exempt from these patterns.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key characteristics
Find the Arabic terms for consistency (الاطراد) and universality (العموم) in the passage. These are explicitly stated as the most prominent characteristics of divine patterns.
The author uses these terms in the first sentence after the subtitle.
2
Locate the evidence for constancy
Find the Quranic verse that directly states that Allah's pattern never changes. This verse (33:62) is cited immediately after introducing the characteristics.
The verse ends with 'وَلَنْ تَجِدَ لِسُنَّةِ اللَّهِ تَبْدِيلاً' – you will never find any change in Allah's pattern.
3
Locate the evidence for universality
Identify the verses and reasoning used to show that patterns apply to all people. The author uses the verse 'إنَّ اللهَ لا يُغَيِّرُ مَا بِقَوْمٍ...' and analyzes the word 'قوم' as indefinite and inclusive. Also note the verse 'لَيْسَ بِأَمَانِيِّكُمْ...' which refutes exclusivity.
The author argues that 'قوم' is a general term covering every group, so no one is exempt.
4
Recognize the implication of no exemption
Understand that even Muslims are not a special case. The author gives the example of the Battle of Uhud where Muslims were defeated because they violated divine patterns, and the response to Jews and Christians who claimed special status.
The point is that patterns apply equally to all, regardless of faith or identity.
5
Apply to Islamic education
Reflect on the author's call for including consistent theories and laws in educational content. This leads to a unified view of existence under divine patterns.
The author stresses that understanding this universality is key to sound Islamic education.

أبرز سمات السنن
وحتى تتحقق الإفادة من هذه السنن النفسية فيجب إدراك أن أبرز سمة لها هي الاطراد والعموم:
وسُنةَ اللهِ فِي الَّذِينَ خَلَوْا مِنْ قَبْلُ وَلَنْ تَجِدَ لِسُنَّةِ اللهِ تَبْدِيلاً [الأحزاب: 62/33].
ولا فرق بين مسلم وغير Muslim في مسألة السنن. فالسنن النفسية تماماً مثل السنن العضوية تنطبق على المسلم والكافر لَيْسَ بِأَمَانِيِّكُمْ وَلَا أَمَانِيِّ أَهْلِ الْكِتَابِ مَنْ يَعْمَلْ سُوءاً يُجْرَ بهِ [النساء: 123/4].
وإن لفظ (قوم) الوارد في قوله تعالى : إنَّ اللهَ لا يُغَيِّرُ ما بِقَوْمٍ حَتَّى يُغَيِّرُوا ما بِأَنفُسِهِمْ [الرعد: 11/13]، يفيد أن السنة عامة مطردة من غير أن تستثني أحداً لاعتبار جنسه أو دينه ف(قوم) نكرة تشمل كل قوم. ومن السذاجة أن يخطر ببال المسلم خصوصية تميزه عن غيره بحيث لا يخضع لسنن الله. ويؤكد هذا قول الله تعالى لرسوله - - : وقُلْ ما كُنتُ بدْعاً مِنَ الرُّسُلِ [الأحقاف: 9/46].
وقول الرسول : التتبعن سنن من كان قبلكم حذو القذة بالقذة...
ولما انهزم المسلمون في أحد فلأنهم خالفوا سنناً في الأنفس، تلك السنن التي لا تحابي أحداً.
وقد رد القرآن على اليهود والنصارى الذين قالوا: نَحْنُ أَبْنَاءُ اللهِ وأحباؤُهُ [المائدة: 18/5] بقوله: ﴿قُلْ فَلِمَ يُعَذِّبُكُمْ بِذُنُوبِكُمْ بَلْ أَنتُمْ بَشَرٌ مِمَّنْ خَلَقَ [المائدة: 118/5]
والحق أن جوهر المشكلة في فهم السنن لا يكمن في العقيدة، بل هي مشكلة فهم لدى العقل المسلم.
على معنى أن فهم المسلمين لهذه الشرعة (السنة) ليست من الوضوح في أذهانهم، بحيث تحول دون النظرات الخاطئة، التي تجعل من المسلم حالة خاصة لا تخضع لما يخضع له عموم البشر.
وهنا يجدر بنا التأكيد على أهمية أن يشمل المحتوى - فيما يشمل- النظريات والقوانين القائمة على الاطّراد، كما يشمل التعميمات المنضبطة انطلاقاً من النظرة الموحدة إلى الوجود كله باعتباره مجموعة من السنن الإلهية، تتعاون وتتآزر لتنتج نظرة واحدة تقود الإنسان إلى الله – سبحانه وتعالى - وهذه هي حقيقة وحدة المعرفة التي تدعو إليها التربية الإسلامية.... وسنشير إلى هذه الوحدة تفصيلاً لاحقاً بإذن الله - تعالى-.
المظاهر السننية
يمثل ميدان الأنفس واقعا مليئًا بمظاهر السنن النفسية والاجتماعية المختلفة، وذلك لأن ميدان الأنفس بحال لإعمال النظر العقلي والحسي: أَوَلَمْ يَتَفَكَّرُوا فِي أَنْفُسِهِمْ [الروم: 8/30]
The most prominent characteristics of the divine patterns (sunan)
And in order to benefit from these psychological patterns, one must realize that their most prominent characteristic is consistency and universality:
"And the pattern of Allah with those who passed on before; and you will never find in the pattern of Allah any change." [Al-Ahzab: 62/33].
There is no difference between Muslim and non-Muslim in the matter of patterns. For psychological patterns, exactly like organic patterns, apply to the Muslim and the disbeliever: "It is not by your wishes nor by the wishes of the People of the Scripture. Whoever does evil will be recompensed for it." [An-Nisa: 123/4].
And indeed the word 'people' (qawm) in His statement, the Exalted: "Indeed Allah does not change the condition of a people until they change what is in themselves" [Ar-Ra'd: 11/13], indicates that the pattern is general and consistent, not exempting anyone due to race or religion. So 'people' is an indefinite noun that includes every people. It is naive for a Muslim to imagine a particularity distinguishing him from others such that he is not subject to the patterns of Allah. And this is affirmed by Allah's statement to His Messenger: "Say: I am not an innovation among the messengers" [Al-Ahqaf: 9/46].
And the saying of the Messenger: "You will surely follow the ways of those before you, handspan by handspan, cubit by cubit..."
And when the Muslims were defeated at Uhud it was because they violated patterns in the selves, those patterns that show favoritism to no one.
And the Qur'an replied to the Jews and Christians who said: "We are the children of Allah and His beloved ones" [Al-Ma'idah: 18/5] with His statement: "Say: 'Then why does He punish you for your sins?' Rather, you are human beings from among those He created." [Al-Ma'idah: 118/5]
And the truth is that the essence of the problem in understanding the patterns does not lie in creed, but rather it is a problem of understanding in the Muslim mind.
Meaning that the Muslims' understanding of this legislation (the pattern) is not clear in their minds, such that it prevents erroneous views that make the Muslim a special case not subject to what the general public is subject to.
And here it is incumbent upon us to emphasize the importance that the content includes - among what it includes - theories and laws based on consistency, as well as disciplined generalizations stemming from a unified view of all existence as a collection of divine patterns, cooperating and synergizing to produce a single vision that leads man to Allah - the Exalted and Majestic - and this is the reality of the unity of knowledge that Islamic education calls for... and we will refer to this unity in detail later, with Allah's permission - the Exalted.
The manifestations of the patterns (al-mazahir al-sunaniyya)
The field of the selves represents a reality full of manifestations of various psychological and social patterns, because the field of the selves is in a state of requiring the exercise of mental and sensory reflection: "Have they not reflected upon themselves?" [Ar-Rum: 8/30]
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is from a modern work on Quranic theory discussing the nature of divine patterns (sunan). The author identifies the most prominent characteristic of these patterns as consistency (al-ittiraad) and universality (al-umoom). He immediately supports this claim with a Quranic verse from Surah Al-Ahzab (33:62) asserting the unchanging nature of Allah's patterns. The author then argues that these patterns apply equally to all humans, Muslims and non-Muslims alike, citing another verse (An-Nisa 4:123) that recompense for evil is based on action, not wishful thinking. To further demonstrate universality, he analyzes the word 'qawm' in the well-known verse from Ar-Ra'd (13:11), noting that it is indefinite and inclusive, thus no group is exempt. He dismisses as naive any notion that Muslims are a special case exempt from divine patterns. This is reinforced by the Prophet's hadith about following the ways of previous nations and the historical example of the Battle of Uhud, where Muslim defeat resulted from violating these patterns. The author then addresses the misconception that this universality is a matter of creed; rather, it is a failure of understanding among Muslims. He concludes by emphasizing the need to include consistent theories and laws in Islamic education, leading to a unified view of existence as governed by divine patterns. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect on the self, quoting Ar-Rum (30:8).

Key Terms
سنة
اطراد
عموم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the most prominent characteristic of divine patterns (sunan)?
A) They only apply to believers.
B) They are unique to each nation.
C) They are consistent and universal.
D) They are subject to change over time.

Key Takeaway
The most prominent characteristics of divine patterns are their consistency and universality, applicable to all people without exception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a current social issue (e.g., poverty, moral decline) and explain how a divine pattern (sunnah) applies to both Muslims and non-Muslims equally, using the passage's reasoning.

Find another Quranic verse that demonstrates the universality of a divine pattern (e.g., the pattern of retribution in Surah Al-Isra 17:15) and explain how it supports the author's argument.

The Field of Knowledge and the Problem of Understanding Divine Laws
Author critiques the misconception that Muslims are exempt from divine laws.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author considers the problem of understanding divine laws to be cognitive rather than creedal.

Core Idea
The real issue is the Muslim mind's misunderstanding, not creed.

The Quran refutes claims of special status, and the author argues that the problem is not with faith but with a flawed understanding that exempts Muslims from universal divine laws. This lesson unpacks that diagnostic claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial objection
Find the claim made by Jews and Christians that the author quotes from the Quran. What special status did they assert?
Refer to Quran 5:18 in the passage.
2
Extract the Quranic response
How does the Quran reply to that claim? Quote the part of 5:118 used.
The response links punishment to sin, showing no exemption.
3
Locate the author's diagnosis
After the Quranic citation, the author states 'the truth is that the essence of the problem...'. What does he identify as the real problem?
He says it is not creedal but a problem of understanding in the Muslim mind.
4
Formulate the problem in your own words
Summarize why the author thinks Muslims misunderstand the sunan, and what consequence this misunderstanding has.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني: الفصل الثاني- ميدان المعرفة - الغيب والشهادة
وقد رد القرآن على اليهود والنصارى الذين قالوا: نَحْنُ أَبْنَاءُ اللهِ وأحباؤُهُ ﴾ [المائدة: ۱۸/۵) بقوله: ﴿قُلْ فَلِمَ يُعَذِّبُكُمْ بِذُنُوبِكُمْ بَلْ أَنتُمْ بَشَرٌ مِمَّنْ خلق له [المائدة: ۱۱۸/۵)
والحق أن جوهر المشكلة في فهم السنن لا يكمن في العقيدة، بل هي مشكلة فهم لدى العقل المسلم.
على معنى أن فهم المسلمين لهذه الشرعة (السنة) ليست من الوضوح في أذهانهم، بحيث تحول دون النظرات الخاطئة، التي تجعل من المسلم حالة خاصة لا تخضع لما يخضع له عموم البشر.
وهنا يجدر بنا التأكيد على أهمية أن يشمل المحتوى - فيما يشمل- النظريات والقوانين القائمة على الاطّراد، كما يشمل التعميمات المنضبطة انطلاقاً من النظرة الموحدة إلى الوجود كله باعتباره مجموعة من السنن الإلهية، تتعاون وتتآزر لتنتج نظرة واحدة تقود الإنسان إلى الله – سبحانه وتعالى - وهذه هي حقيقة وحدة المعرفة التي تدعو إليها التربية الإسلامية.... وسنشير إلى هذه الوحدة تفصيلاً لاحقاً بإذن الله - تعالى-.
المظاهر السننية
يمثل ميدان الأنفس واقعا مليئًا بمظاهر السنن النفسية والاجتماعية المختلفة، وذلك لأن ميدان الأنفس بحال لإعمال النظر العقلي والحسي: أَوَلَمْ يَتَفَكَّرُوا فِي أَنْفُسِهِمْ ﴾ [الروم: ٨/٣٠]
﴿وَفِي أَنْفُسِكُمْ أَفَلا تُبْصِرُونَ ﴾ [الذاريات: ٢١/٥١]
وقد أشار القرآن إلى بعض مظاهر السنن تلك في مواضع متفرقة من الكتاب العزيز، وعلى سبيل المثال:
١ - مظهر السببية
إن مظهر السببية أو العلية مظهر شاخص بجلاء في ميدان الأنفس - بصرف النظر عن الجدل الدائر في تفصيلاته - فإنه إلى جانب الحركة يجسد آية من آيات الله في نظامه الكوني، الذي يخضع له الإنسان في مادته بوصف ذلك سنة ثابتة لا يستطيع الفكاك منها. وهذا ما يؤكده قول الله تعالى:
﴿وَإِن مِنْ شَيْءٍ إِلا يُسَبِّحُ بِحَمْدِهِ وَلَكِنْ لا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ [الإسراء: ٤٤/١٧].
وقوله : وإِن كُلُّ مَنْ فِي السَّماواتِ وَالْأَرْضِ إِلَّا آتِي الرَّحْمَنِ عَبْدا [مريم: ۹۳/۱۹].
فالتسبيح والعبودية هما تعبير عن النظام الثابت الذي تخضع له كل الكائنات بما فيها الإنسان.
وتندرج ظاهرة السببية في إطار الخلق الهادف الذي يتضح من خلاله قانون السبب والمسبب وبه يهتدي الإنسان إلى معرفة خالقه بصفة من صفاته أو فعل من أفعاله.
ونجد القرآن الكريم يقرر هذا المظهر في غير موضع، وفي أكثر من محال. فمظهر السببية يقضي بأن تكون النتائج من جنس المقدمات عند محاكمة
The second chapter, the second section: The Field of Knowledge - The Unseen and the Witnessed. And the Quran responded to the Jews and Christians who said, 'We are the sons of God and His beloved' [5:18] with His saying: 'Say, then why does He punish you for your sins? Rather, you are human beings from among those He created' [5:118]. And the truth is that the essence of the problem in understanding the divine laws does not lie in creed, but rather it is a problem of understanding for the Muslim mind. Meaning that the Muslims' understanding of this law (the Sunnah) is not clear in their minds, such that it prevents mistaken views that make the Muslim a special case not subject to what all humans are subject to. Here it is incumbent upon us to emphasize the importance that the content includes - among what it includes - theories and laws based on regularity, as well as controlled generalizations stemming from a unified view of all existence as a collection of divine laws that cooperate and synergize to produce a single view that leads man to God - glorified and exalted - and this is the reality of the unity of knowledge that Islamic education calls for... and we will refer to this unity in detail later, God willing. The manifestations of the laws. The realm of the self represents a reality filled with manifestations of various psychological and social laws, because the realm of the self is in a state of engaging rational and sensory reflection: 'Do they not reflect upon themselves?' [30:8] 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' [51:21]. And the Quran has indicated some of those manifestations of laws in various places of the Noble Book, for example: 1 - The manifestation of causality. Indeed, the manifestation of causality or efficient causation is a clear manifestation in the realm of the self - regardless of the debate about its details - for it, along with motion, embodies a sign of God's signs in His cosmic system, to which man is subject in his material aspect, as that is a fixed law from which he cannot escape. And this is affirmed by the saying of God Almighty: 'And there is not a thing except that it glorifies His praise, but you do not understand their glorification' [17:44]. And His saying: 'And there is none in the heavens and the earth but that he comes to the Most Merciful as a servant' [19:93]. So glorification and servitude are expressions of the fixed system to which all beings, including man, are subject. And the phenomenon of causality falls within the framework of purposeful creation through which the law of cause and effect becomes clear, and by it man is guided to knowledge of his Creator by one of His attributes or one of His actions. And we find the Holy Quran affirming this manifestation in more than one place, and in more than one context. For the manifestation of causality requires that results be of the same kind as premises when judging.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological definition that establishes the basis for understanding divine laws (sunan) in the Quranic worldview. It begins by refuting the claim of Jews and Christians that they are special children of God, citing Quran 5:18 and 5:118, to show that no one is exempt from divine accountability. The author then argues that the real problem is not creedal but cognitive: Muslims have not properly understood that the sunan apply universally, including to themselves. This misunderstanding leads to the false notion that Muslims are exceptions to general rules. The passage then emphasizes the need for a unified view of existence as a system of divine laws (sunan ilahiyya) that are regular (muttarida) and controlled (mundabita), leading to a single worldview that guides to God. This is the core of Islamic education's call for unity of knowledge. The author then introduces 'the manifestations of the laws' (al-mazahir al-sunaniyya), focusing on the realm of the self (midan al-anfus) as a field full of psychological and social laws, accessible by rational and sensory reflection, supported by Quranic verses urging self-reflection. As the first example, the manifestation of causality (mazhar al-sababiyya) is presented as a clear sign in the cosmic system, a fixed law to which humans are subject. The author supports this with Quranic verses about universal glorification and servitude, indicating a fixed order. Causality is then situated within purposeful creation (khalq hadif), where the law of cause and effect guides humans to knowledge of God. The passage concludes by reaffirming that the Quran repeatedly affirms this manifestation, and that causality requires that results be of the same kind as premises in judgment. This is a foundational concept for understanding how the Quran presents a law-governed universe.

Key Terms
السنن الإلهية
ميدان المعرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the real problem with Muslims' understanding of divine laws?
A) Their creed is incorrect.
B) Their understanding is unclear, leading to the false belief that they are exceptions.
C) They do not read the Quran enough.
D) The divine laws are not meant for Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The problem of understanding divine laws is cognitive, not creedal – Muslims must recognize that no one is exempt from the universal sunan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a contemporary Muslim misconception that fits the author's description of 'making the Muslim a special case not subject to what all humans are subject to.' Provide one example and explain how it violates the principle of sunan.
